PINNING IN NON-CRITICAL HALF-SPACE GEOMETRIC LAST PASSAGE
PERCOLATION
SAYAN DAS, EVGENI DIMITROV, AND ZONGRUI YANG
Abstract. We study a symmetrized (half-space) version of geometric last passage percolation with
a boundary parameter c that interpolates between subcritical, critical, and supercritical behavior.
This model gives rise to a family of interlacing random curves, or a line ensemble, which encode
both the usual last passage time and its higher-rank analogues.
Although these ensembles are
understood in most spaceвАУtime regions, their behavior near the diagonalвАФwhere the boundary
effects are strongestвАФhas remained unclear outside the critical regime.
We determine the universal scaling limits of the line ensemble in this near-diagonal region for
both subcritical (c < 1) and supercritical (c > 1) phases. In the subcritical case, after appropriate
centering and scaling, the entire line ensemble converges to the pinned half-space Airy line ensemble,
a universal Brownian Gibbsian object recently constructed as a canonical limit for half-space models
in the KPZ universality class in arXiv:2601.04546. In the supercritical case, we prove an analogous
convergence together with a curve-separation phenomenon: the lower curves converge to the same
pinned half-space Airy limit, while the top curve decouples and converges to Brownian motion.
These results essentially complete the asymptotic description of half-space geometric last passage
percolation and provide a new rigorous instance of the pinned half-space Airy line ensemble as a
universal scaling limit.

1. Introduction and main results
1.1. Half-space geometric last passage percolation. We begin by introducing symmetrized
geometric last passage percolation (LPP). The model depends on two parameters q вИИ(0, 1) and
c вИИ[0, qвИТ1). Let W = (wi,j : i, j вЙ•1) be an array such that the collection (wi,j : 1 вЙ§i вЙ§j) consists
of independent geometric random variables, with wi,j вИЉGeom(q2) when i ћЄ= j and wi,i вИЉGeom(cq).
We impose the symmetry constraint wi,j = wj,i for all i, j вЙ•1. Here, we write X вИЉGeom(ќ±) to
mean P(X = k) = ќ±k(1 вИТќ±) for k вИИZвЙ•0.
In other words, the entries of W are independent
geometric random variables, conditioned to form a symmetric matrix.

The symmetry wi,j = wj,i immediately implies that G1(m, n) = G1(n, m) for all m, n. Conse-
quently, it suffices to study the values of G1 in the region n вЙ•m вЙ•1 (or symmetrically m вЙ•n вЙ•1).
Thus, all information about the model is contained in an octant rather than the full quadrant. For
this reason, the model is often referred to as half-space LPP.
When viewed on this octant, the diagonal {(m, m) : m вЙ•1} plays the role of a boundary. The
parameter c governs the distribution of the weights along this boundary and is therefore called a
boundary parameter. Larger values of c correspond to heavier weights on the diagonal, while smaller
values correspond to lighter ones. The value c = 1 is critical, and the model exhibits qualitatively
different behavior when transitioning from the subcritical regime c вИИ[0, 1) to the supercritical
regime c вИИ(1, qвИТ1). Because of this phase transition, it is natural to study the asymptotic behavior
of G1(m, n) when c is fixed in either regime or when c is tuned near criticality as c = 1вИТѕЦќ±qnвИТ1/3.
Here, ќ±q is a q-dependent constant defined differently in different works, and ѕЦ вИИR is fixed.
The first asymptotic results for symmetrized LPP were obtained by Baik and Rains [BR01c].
Building on their earlier studies [BR01a, BR01b], they proved that the random variable G1(n, n)
has fluctuations governed by the GOE Tracy-Widom distribution FGOE when c = 1, and by the GSE
Tracy-Widom distribution FGSE when c вИИ[0, 1). The precise definitions of these distributions can be
found in [TW05]. They also showed that if c = 1 вИТѕЦќ±qnвИТ1/3, then the fluctuations of G1(n, n) are
described asymptotically by a family of cross-over distributions Fcross(¬Ј; ѕЦ) interpolating between
FGOE when ѕЦ = 0, FGSE when ѕЦ вЖТвИЮ, and the normal distribution ќ¶ when ѕЦ вЖТвИТвИЮ.
3
In subsequent work [SI04], Imamura and Sasamoto investigated the related polynuclear growth
model (PNG) at the special parameter values c = 0 and c = 1. For any fixed ќЇ вИИ(0, 1), they proved
that, after suitable scaling, the joint distributions of G1(t, n) for t near ќЇn converge to those of
the Airy process. They also analyzed the finite-dimensional distributions of G1(t, n) for t near n
and expressed their limits as Fredholm Pfaffians, which differ in the cases c = 0 and c = 1. Later,
Betea, Bouttier, Nejjar, and VuletiƒЗ [BBNV18] computed the corresponding limits of G1(t, n) when
c = 1 вИТѕЦќ±qnвИТ1/3, obtaining a one-parameter family of Fredholm Pfaffians indexed by ѕЦ. The
expressions discovered in [SI04] for c = 0 and c = 1 arise as the special cases ѕЦ = 0 and ѕЦ вЖТвИЮ,
respectively. The formulas in [BBNV18] were also obtained earlier by Baik, Barraquand, Corwin,
and Suidan in the context of LPP with exponential weights [BBCS18].

k вЙ•1) of higher-rank last passage times, which we now describe. For k = 1, . . . , min(m, n), let

The line ensembles {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 have attracted considerable attention in recent years, and we
summarize the main results concerning them in the next few paragraphs, see also Figure 3. Exact
formulas are available for these ensembles separately in the regions t вИИ[0, n] and t вИИ[n, вИЮ), and
analyses in the literature have typically focused on only one of these intervals at a time. However,
because the formulas in the two regions are structurally similar, once a certain behavior is established
in one interval, one expects that an analogous argument yields the corresponding result in the other.

Figure 3. Phase diagram for {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1. The top and bottom panels correspond
to the subcritical c вИИ[0, 1) and supercritical c вИИ(1, qвИТ1) regimes, respectively.
Subcritical regime. In [Zho26], Zhou showed that when c вИИ(0, 1) and ќЇ вИИ(0, 1), the line ensem-
bles {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 with t near ќЇn converge (after an appropriate shift and scaling) to the Airy line
ensemble introduced in [CH14]. This corresponds to region I.a in the top half of Figure 3, and an
analogous statement is expected to hold for region I.b.
Supercritical regime. When c вИИ(1, qвИТ1), the model develops several regions exhibiting distinct
behaviors (see the bottom half of Figure 3). These regions are encoded by the intervals (0, ќЇвИЧ),
(ќЇвИЧ, 1), (1, ќЇвИЧ), and (ќЇвИЧ, вИЮ), corresponding to I.a, III.a, III.b, and I.b, respectively. The constants
ќЇвИЧand ќЇвИЧare given explicitly by
(1.7)

(1 вИТqc)2 .
In addition, one observes different behaviors at the transition points ќЇвИЧ, 1, and ќЇвИЧ, corresponding to
IV.a, II.ab, and IV.b, respectively. We note that II.a and II.b correspond to the regions immediately
to the left and right of 1. The two sides can be analyzed separately, but their joint analysis remains
out of reach due to a lack of exact formulas.
5
In [Zho26], Zhou showed that when c вИИ(1, qвИТ1) and ќЇ вИИ(0, ќЇвИЧ), corresponding to region I.a,
the fluctuations of the line ensembles {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 with t near ќЇn are described by the Airy line
ensemble.
A similar result is expected to hold when ќЇ вИИ(ќЇвИЧ, вИЮ), corresponding to region I.b.
Moreover, when ќЇ = ќЇвИЧ, corresponding to IV.a, the same paper showed that the fluctuations are
governed asymptotically by the Airy wanderer line ensemble from [AFVM10, CH14, Dim26].
A
similar statement is expected to hold when ќЇ = ќЇвИЧ, corresponding to IV.b.
The behavior when ќЇ вИИ(ќЇвИЧ, 1), corresponding to III.a, was investigated by the second author and
Zhou in [DZ25], where it was shown that the ensemble undergoes a curve-separation phenomenon.
Specifically, what happens on [ќЇвИЧn, n] is that the top curve ќї1(t, n) separates from the rest and be-
haves like a Brownian motion. On the other hand, the remaining curves {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•2 converge for
t near ќЇn (after a suitable shift and scaling) to the Airy line ensemble. A similar result is expected
to hold in region III.b, that is, when ќЇ вИИ(1, ќЇвИЧ).
Critical regime. In [DY25a], the second and third authors investigated the behavior of {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1
for t near and to the right of n, corresponding to region II.b, and c = 1 вИТѕЦќ±qnвИТ1/3. In this case,
the limiting behavior is described by a one-parameter family (indexed by ѕЦ) of line ensembles
Ahs;ѕЦ = {Ahs;ѕЦ
k
}kвЙ•1, called the (critical) half-space Airy line ensembles. The latter are natural
half-space analogues of the classical Airy line ensemble and are expected to arise as universal scal-
ing limits of critical half-space models in the Kardar-Parisi-Zhang (KPZ) universality class.
A
similar result is expected to hold in region II.a.
The aforementioned works give a fairly complete description of the line ensembles {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1,
but an important gap remains: their behavior when t is near n in both the subcritical and su-
percritical regimes, corresponding to II.ab in Figure 3. In this region, {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 naturally fit
into the framework of half-space Gibbsian line ensembles, which was introduced by Barraquand,
Corwin, and the first author in [BCD24]. That work proposed the existence of a pinned half-space
Airy line ensemble, expected to describe the scaling limit of various subcritical half-space models
in the KPZ universality class. It was further conjectured that this ensemble is locally described by
ordered, pairwise pinned Brownian motions; see [BCD24, Figure 2(B)] or the right side of Figure
4 for a discrete illustration of this pinning phenomenon. This property was formalized by the first
author in joint work with Serio [DS25b] and is now referred to as the pinned half-space Brownian
Gibbs property. Very recently, the second and third authors, together with Serio [DSY26], formally
constructed the pinned half-space Airy line ensemble, denoted Ahs;вИЮ= {Ahs;вИЮ
k
}kвЙ•1. Specifically,
they defined Ahs;вИЮas the weak ѕЦ вЖТвИЮlimit of the critical half-space Airy line ensembles Ahs;ѕЦ
from [DY25a], and established several fundamental properties of this new object.
The main goal of the present paper is to show that the pinned half-space Airy line ensem-
ble Ahs;вИЮgoverns the scaling-limit fluctuations of {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 for t near and to the right of n,
corresponding to region II.b. More precisely, in the subcritical regime c вИИ[0, 1), we prove that
the appropriately shifted and scaled curves {ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 converge weakly to Ahs;вИЮ; see Theorem
1.5(a). In the supercritical regime c вИИ(1, qвИТ1), we establish an analogous convergence together with
a curve-separation phenomenon similar to that observed in [DZ25]. In this case, the lower curves
{ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•2 converge to Ahs;вИЮ, while the top curve ќї1(t, n) decouples and converges to a (time-
reversed) Brownian motion; see Theorems 1.5(b) and 1.11. These results complete the analysis of
{ќїk(t, n)}kвЙ•1 in region II.b for both phases. We expect analogous behavior in region II.a, but do
not pursue this direction here.

1.3. Key ideas and paper outline. In order to prove Theorems 1.5 and 1.11, we follow a general
two-step strategy:
I. Establish finite-dimensional convergence of the sequence of random curves.
II. Prove that the sequence is tight in the appropriate space of continuous functions, endowed
with the topology of uniform convergence over compact sets.
The key ingredient underlying both steps is a distributional equality between half-space LPP
and the Pfaffian Schur processes introduced in [BR05]. These processes are defined in Section 4.1,
and their connection to LPP is established in Proposition 4.3. Once this identity is shown, we can
leverage the structural properties of the Pfaffian Schur processes to verify each of the two steps
above, as we explain next.
We begin with the finite-dimensional convergence. A central feature of the Pfaffian Schur pro-
cesses is that they form Pfaffian point processes with an explicit correlation kernel, given by double
contour integrals. The relevant formula is recalled from [BR05] in Proposition 4.4. In Lemmas 4.11
and 4.15, we derive two alternative representations of the correlation kernel that are well suited for
asymptotic analysis in the two scaling regimes corresponding to Theorems 1.5 and 1.11. In Section
5, we show pointwise convergence of these kernels using the method of steepest descent. The precise
statements are given in Propositions 5.1 and 5.10, whose proofs rely on suitable adaptations of
arguments from [DY25a] and [DZ25].
Under general conditions, convergence of the correlation kernels implies convergence of the asso-
ciated point processes in the vague topology (see [DY25a]). To strengthen this to finite-dimensional
convergence, we employ a general framework from [Dim26], which reduces the problem to estab-
lishing one-point tightness from above for our curves. This is carried out in Section 7.1 for the
scaling in Theorem 1.5, and in Section 6.1 for the scaling in Theorem 1.11. The finite-dimensional
convergence itself is then proved in Proposition 7.2 in Section 7.2 (for the scaling in Theorem 1.5),
and in Proposition 6.3 in Section 6.2 (for the scaling in Theorem 1.11).
We next turn to the proof of tightness for our curves, i.e., the second step above.
The key
property we exploit is that a Pfaffian Schur process admits a representation as a product of skew
Schur symmetric functions. This leads to the following formula for any partitions ќї0, . . . , ќїM:

work, tightness of the ensembles was established via a strong comparison between the geometric
random walk trajectories and Brownian motions, using a version of the Koml√≥s-Major-Tusn√°dy

In contrast, when c ћЄ= 1, this approach breaks down as the interaction at the origin becomes too
strong, and the curves can no longer be treated individually. At a heuristic level, when c вИИ[0, 1), the
odd-indexed curves develop a large negative drift and tend to escape to вИТвИЮ, while the even-indexed
curves develop a large positive drift and tend to escape to +вИЮ. However, due to the interlacing
constraints, the upward-moving curves end up colliding with the negatively-moving ones, ultimately
producing the pinning phenomenon at the origin. When c вИИ(1, qвИТ1), a similar pairwise collision
occurs, except that the top curve does escape to +вИЮas there is no curve above it.

We view Theorem 3.8 as one of the main technical contributions of the paper.

1.4. Related works and future directions. Our work fits into the broader program of studying
scaling limits of half-space integrable models, and provides the first concrete example of convergence
to the pinned half-space Airy line ensemble introduced in [DSY26].
From a technical perspective, our approach is closest to [DY25a] and [DZ25]. As discussed in
Section 1.1, [DY25a] analyzes the same model in the regime when c is critically scaled around 1.
While the asymptotic analysis leading to finite-dimensional convergence is very similar to ours, the
tightness argument in [DY25a] is considerably simpler. As we explain in Section 1.3, the main
additional difficulty in the present work arises from the pinning of the curves, which prevents one
from treating each curve individually via a comparison with avoiding Brownian motions or Brownian
bridges. To overcome this, we instead group consecutive curves into pairs and compare each pair to
what we call a pinned pair of avoiding reverse Brownian motions. At the level of the full ensemble,
this leads to a comparison with a sequence of avoiding pinned pairs, which we term a pinned reverse
Brownian line ensemble. Furthermore, in the supercritical regime c > 1, the top curve in our model
escapes to +вИЮ, which introduces additional challenges compared to [DY25a]; these can be handled
using ideas from [DZ25].
Beyond [DY25a] and [DZ25], there has been substantial recent progress on positive-temperature
analogues of our model. In the seminal work [BCD24], Barraquand, Corwin, and the first author
initiated a systematic study of general half-space Gibbsian line ensembles, a class that includes
11
our LPP model as a special case.
Although [BCD24] focuses primarily on the half-space log-
gamma polymer, it introduced several key ideas that have since driven further developments; see
[DS25a,DZ24,Gin24] and the references therein. Using the geometric RSK correspondence [COSZ14,
OSZ14,NZ17,BZ19], they showed that the free energy process of the half-space log-gamma polymer
can be embedded as the lowest-indexed curve of a half-space log-gamma line ensemble. In [BCD24],
they proved tightness of the top curve under 1:2:3 KPZ scaling in the critical and subcritical regimes,
and this was later extended to tightness of all curves in [DS25a]. Similar results were subsequently
obtained for the half-space KPZ equation in [DS25b].
In contrast, much less is known in the supercritical regime c > 1 regarding process-level limits of
the half-space log-gamma line ensemble and the half-space KPZ equation. In this direction, [DZ24]
established a weak form of curve separation for the half-space log-gamma polymer, which allowed
them to conclude Gaussian fluctuations in an o(
вИЪ
N) window around the boundary. Later, [Gin24]
proved analogous Gaussian behavior at the boundary for general (non-integrable) half-space LPP
and polymer models under a law of large numbers separation hypothesis.
One of the main reasons that [DS25a] and [DS25b] establish only tightness (rather than full
convergence) is the lack of sufficiently tractable exact formulas. Although the half-space log-gamma
polymer model is integrable via its connection to the half-space Whittaker process [BBC20], the
resulting formulas are not expressed in terms of Pfaffian point processes and are not readily amenable
to asymptotic analysis.
At present, pointwise convergence is known only at the boundary, due
to [IMS26], which relates the half-space model to a free-boundary version of the Schur process.
Nevertheless, it is widely expected that these models should converge to the same pinned half-space
Airy line ensemble limit.
A key advantage of the present work is the integrable structure of the model: it admits a Pfaffian
point process representation with an explicit correlation kernel given by double contour integrals.
This provides access to exact formulas which, although still requiring substantial analysis, allow us
to obtain a detailed description of the convergence of all curves of our ensemble. We expect that our
techniques can be extended to other integrable half-space models, such as exponential, Bernoulli,
Poisson, or Brownian LPP.
While positive-temperature models like the log-gamma polymer are not known to admit a Pfaffian
point process structure, we anticipate that several of the techniques developed here вАФ particularly
those related to line ensembles вАФ can be adapted to that setting. For instance, the aforementioned
convergence to a pinned reverse Brownian line ensemble, see Lemma 3.6 for a precise statement,
relies only minimally on integrable input and should extend to the broader framework of Macdonald
processes, which include the log-gamma polymer as a degeneration. To complete the proof of weak
convergence to the pinned Airy line ensemble in such settings, a promising direction would be to
establish a strong characterization for this object, analogous to the one recently obtained for the
(full-space) Airy line ensemble in [AH25].

Simons Award TSM-00014004. Z.Y. was partially supported by Ivan CorwinвАЩs NSF grants DMS:

Minerva FellowsвАЩ program.
2. Gibbsian line ensembles
As mentioned in Section 1.3, we establish tightness of the curves in Theorems 1.5 and 1.11 by
exploiting the Gibbs property of the line ensemble LLPP;N. In this section, we introduce the necessary
notation and results to formulate this property precisely and to carry out the tightness proof later
in the paper.
2.1. Brownian line ensembles. In this section, we recall some basic definitions and notation
regarding line ensembles, largely following [DFF+21, Section 2].

Our goal for the remainder of this section is to introduce the pinned half-space Brownian Gibbs
property. This property was originally introduced by the first author and Serio [DS25b], although
our discussion below will be closer to [DSY26, Section 4.1]. We mention that in [DSY26, Section
4.1] the property was formulated in terms of 3D Bessel bridges, but it will be convenient for us to
rephrase it in terms of the measures Pa,b,¬µ

between the two.

2.2. Geometric line ensembles. In this section, we introduce several discrete analogues of the
definitions in Section 2.1. Our discussion largely follows [Dim24, Section 2.1].
Definition 2.17. Let ќ£ вКЖZ and JT0, T1K be a nonempty integer interval in Z. Consider the set Y of
functions f : ќ£ √Ч JT0, T1K вЖТZ such that f(i, j + 1) вИТf(i, j) вИИZвЙ•0 when i вИИќ£ and j вИИJT0, T1 вИТ1K
and let D denote the discrete ѕГ-algebra on Y .
We call a function f : JT0, T1K вЖТZ such that
f(j + 1) вИТf(j) вИИZвЙ•0 when j вИИJT0, T1 вИТ1K an increasing path and elements in Y collections of
increasing paths. A ќ£-indexed geometric line ensemble L on JT0, T1K is a random variable defined on
17
a probability space (вД¶, B, P), taking values in Y such that L is a (B, D)-measurable function. Unless
otherwise specified, we will assume that T0 вЙ§T1 are both integers, although the above definition
makes sense if T0 = вИТвИЮ, or T1 = вИЮ, or both.
Remark 2.18. The condition f(i, j + 1) вИТf(i, j) вИИZвЙ•0 when i вИИќ£ and j вИИJT0, T1 вИТ1K essentially
means that for each i вИИќ£ the function f(i, ¬Ј) can be thought of as the trajectory of a geometric
random walk from time T0 to time T1. Here, and throughout the paper, a geometric random variable
X with parameter q вИИ(0, 1) has probability mass function P(X = k) = (1 вИТq)qk for k вИИZвЙ•0.
We think of geometric line ensembles as collections of random increasing paths on the integer
lattice, indexed by ќ£. Observe that one can view an increasing path L on JT0, T1K as a continuous
curve by linearly interpolating the points (i, L(i)), see Figure 2. This allows us to define (L(ѕЙ))(i, s)
for non-integer s вИИ[T0, T1] and to view geometric line ensembles as line ensembles in the sense of
Definition 2.1. In particular, we can think of L as a random element in C(ќ£ √Ч ќЫ) with ќЫ = [T0, T1].
We will often slightly abuse notation and write L : ќ£ √Ч JT0, T1K вЖТZ, even though it is not L
which is such a function, but rather L(ѕЙ) for each ѕЙ вИИвД¶. Furthermore, we write Li = (L(ѕЙ))(i, ¬Ј)
for the index i вИИќ£ path. If L is an increasing path on JT0, T1K and a, b вИИJT0, T1K satisfy a вЙ§b we
let LJa, bK denote the restriction of L to Ja, bK.

3. Properties of geometric line ensembles
The main result of this section, Theorem 3.8, provides a general tightness criterion for sequences
of geometric line ensembles that satisfy the interacting pair Gibbs property of Definition 2.27. This
criterion serves as the key input for establishing tightness in Theorem 1.5. It is stated in Section 3.3,
and its proof, which occupies Section 3.4, relies on several technical results concerning geometric
line ensembles that are established in Sections 3.1 and 3.2. Throughout this section, we continue to
use the notation introduced in Section 2.
3.1. Convergence of geometric line ensembles. The goal of this section is to show that, under
appropriate scaling, the ensembles from Definition 2.24 converge weakly to those from Definition
2.11. The precise statement is given in Lemma 3.6 below, which is the main result of this section.
We begin by establishing several statements we require for its proof.
The following lemma shows that geometric line ensembles with laws PT0,T1,вГЧx,вГЧy
Inter,Geom as in Definition
2.19 can be monotonically coupled in their boundary data. Its proof is given in Section A.3.

The next lemma shows that, under appropriate scaling, a sequence of (вИЮ, вИТвИЮ)-interlacing geo-

Remark 3.9. The proof of Theorem 3.8 is given in Section 3.4 and follows closely that of [Dim24,

interlacing Gibbs property. Consequently, as we explain in Section 3.4, we conclude that Ln satisfies

Theorem 3.8 strengthens this statement so that tightness is established on the whole interval [0, ќ≤).

half-space Brownian Gibbs property.

statement. In Step 2, we show that at a fixed positive time the curves of Ln are likely to separate
from each other and be bounded with high probability. In Step 3, we prove the tightness part of
Theorem 3.8, and in Step 4, we prove that any subsequential limit LвИЮof Ln satisfies the pinned
half-space Brownian Gibbs property on ќЫ = [0, ќ≤).

4. Pfaffian Schur processes
In this section, we introduce a family of Pfaffian Schur processes and discuss some of their
properties. These models are special cases of the processes introduced in [BR05], which in turn
are Pfaffian analogs of the determinantal Schur processes introduced in [OR03]. Our exposition
largely follows [DY25a, Section 5.3], and we refer the reader to [BR05,BBNV18,BBCS18] for further
background on Pfaffian Schur processes. Throughout this section, we freely use the definitions and
notation concerning Pfaffian point processes from [DY25a, Section 5.2]. We also refer the reader
to [OQR17, Appendix B] and [Rai00] for additional background on Pfaffian point processes.
4.1. Definitions. A partition is a non-increasing sequence of non-negative integers ќї = (ќї1 вЙ•
ќї2 вЙ•. . . ) with finitely many non-zero elements.
For any partition ќї, we denote its weight by

5. Kernel asymptotics

Proof. The proof is very similar to that of [DY25a, Lemma 6.4]. For clarity, we split the proof into
five steps. In the first step, we collect some estimates from [DY25a, Section 7.1] for the functions
appearing in the integrands of IN
ij and RN
ij from Lemma 4.11, which will be useful in the proof. In
the second step, we show that we can truncate the contours ќ≥¬±

ij and
RN
ij to ќ≥¬±
N(¬±1, 0). The effect of this truncation is asymptotically negligible. In the third step, we
prove (5.1), (5.2) and (5.3), in the fourth step we prove (5.4), and in the fifth step we prove (5.5).
We will use a change of variables that brings the truncated contours ќ≥¬±

which the integrands will have pointwise limits. We then obtain bounds that allow us to use the
dominated convergence theorem, from which we conclude the limits of the integrals.

sufficiently large so that xN, yN вИИ[вИТA, A].

6. Convergence to Brownian motion
The goal of this section is to prove Theorem 1.11. The proof is given in Section 6.4, and follows
the general outline below:
(1) Definition 6.4 introduces a discrete line ensemble LN = (LN
1 , LN
2 ) and its scaled version
LN = (LN
1 , LN
2 ), related to the Pfaffian Schur processes from Definition 4.1. Proposition 4.3
implies that under an appropriate restriction Utop,N
1
d= LN
1 .
(2) Proposition 6.5 establishes that the sequence {LN
1 }NвЙ•1 is tight.
(3) We then show that all subsequential limits of LN
1 share the same finite-dimensional distri-
butions as W. Combined with point (2), this implies LN
1 вЗТW. Hence, by point (1), we
conclude Utop,N
1
вЗТW, establishing Theorem 1.11.
The first point above holds directly from the definition of LN. To establish the third point, we
proceed as follows:
(3A) The vectors {Y j,N
1
: j вИИJ1, mK} from Definition 4.13 converge weakly to a vector of Brownian
motion marginals, see Proposition 6.3.
(3B) The Y j,N
i
are close to the corresponding scaled marginals of LN
i , see (6.34).
Point (3A) is established using the general framework from [DZ25], which requires that (3A.1)
{Y j,N
1
}NвЙ•1 is tight from above (shown in Lemma 6.1), and (3A.2) the measures M N from Lemma
4.15 converge weakly (established in Step 1 of the proof of Proposition 6.3, using Lemma 5.10).
Lastly, to establish the second point, we show the following:
(2A) The second curve LN
2 is typically much lower than the top curve LN
1 вАУ see Lemma 6.7 for a
precise statement.
(2B) The curve LN
1 has a well-controlled modulus of continuity when LN
2 is very low.
Point (2A) above is established by showing that (2A.1) LN
2
is likely very low at finitely many
times (this is implied by the technical Lemma 6.2 and the finite-dimensional convergence result
Proposition 6.3), and (2A.2) LN
2 is increasing (this allows us to control LN
2 uniformly by controlling
it at finitely many points). Point (2B) is established by showing that (2B.1) LN
1 behaves like a
geometric random walk bridge interlacing with LN
2 (see Lemma 6.6 for a precise statement), and
(2B.2) a geometric random walk bridge has a well-behaved modulus of continuity (this follows from
a strong coupling with Brownian bridges, see Proposition 6.8).
The remainder of this section provides the detailed arguments supporting the above outline.
57
6.1. Upper tail probability estimates. In this section, we establish two results вАУ Lemmas 6.1
and 6.2 вАУ which show that the variables Y j,N
i
from Definition 4.13 are unlikely to be very large.
Lemma 6.1. Assume the same notation as in Definition 4.13. For each j вИИJ1, mK, we have
(6.1)
lim
aвЖТвИЮlim sup
NвЖТвИЮ
P(Y j,N

6.4. Proof of Theorem 1.11. We continue with the notation of the theorem. For a function f,
denote by f[a, b] its restriction to the interval [a, b].

1

7. Convergence to the pinned Airy line ensemble
The goal of this section is to prove Theorem 1.5. In Section 7.1 we establish certain upper tail
estimates, see Lemma 7.1, that in Section 7.2 are used to establish a finite-dimensional analogue
of Theorem 1.5, see Proposition 7.2. The two parts of Theorem 1.5 are proved in Sections 7.3 and
7.4 by combining the finite-dimensional convergence of Proposition 7.2 and the tightness criterion
of Theorem 3.8.

Appendix A. Technical lemmas
In this section we establish several auxiliary results used in the main text. Specifically, we prove
Lemma 2.22 in Section A.1, Lemma 3.2 in Section A.2, and Lemma 3.1 in Section A.3.

We end this section with the proof of Lemma 2.22.
Proof of Lemma 2.22. As q вИИ(0, 1) and c вИИ[0, qвИТ1), we observe directly from (A.1) that W(B; q, c) вЙ•
0 for all B = (B1, B2) вИИвД¶Inter(T1, вГЧy). In particular, Z(T1, вГЧy; q, c) вИИ[0, вИЮ]. From Lemma A.1, we
know Z(T1, вГЧy; q, c) < вИЮ. Lastly, define Q = (Q1, Q2) by
Q1(s) = Q2(s) = y2 for s вИИJ0, T1 вИТ1K, and (Q1(T1), Q2(T1)) = (y1, y2).
Observe that Q вИИвД¶Inter(T1, вГЧy) and W(Q; q, c) = qy1вИТy2 > 0, which implies Z(T1, вГЧy; q, c) > 0.
